Jake Paul on Going From YouTube to Boxing to Investing | a16z ft. Anti Fund

a16z(278.0K)·65:58·4.1K views·2026-06-22
Jake PaulCo-Founder, Anti Fund Geoff WooCo-Founder, Anti Fund
TL;DRAnti Fund, co-founded by Jake Paul and Geoff Woo, has launched an oversubscribed $100 million growth fund targeting marquee AI, defense, and deep tech companies, leveraging Paul’s cultural distribution engine and Woo’s analytical venture strategy.
Fund Launch: Anti...

TL;DR

Anti Fund, co-founded by Jake Paul and Geoff Woo, has launched an oversubscribed $100 million growth fund targeting marquee AI, defense, and deep tech companies, leveraging Paul’s cultural distribution engine and Woo’s analytical venture strategy.

The $100M Growth Fund & Portfolio Strategy

  • Fund Launch: Anti Fund has officially announced its $100 million growth fund, focusing on elite, high-conviction investments.
  • Tier-One Portfolio: The fund’s portfolio includes industry-defining companies such as SpaceX, OpenAI, Anthropic, Anduril, Cognition, Etched, Modal, and Saronic.
  • Institutional Backing: Silicon Valley pioneers Marc Andreessen and Chris Dixon (a16z) were among the fund's very first LPs, validating the duo’s strategy early on.
  • A Unique Partnership: The fund leverages the contrasting yet complementary skill sets of its founders:
      • Geoff Woo: Brings computer science rigor (Stanford background) and deep analytical venture expertise.
      • Jake Paul: Acts as a generational tastemaker with a massive, direct-to-consumer attention engine that provides portfolio companies with unparalleled cultural reach.

Attention as a Scarce Asset in the AI Era

  • "AI-Maxing" vs. "Looksmaxing": As artificial intelligence commoditizes raw intelligence, human-centric assets—such as personal branding, charisma, and attention—become the ultimate economic differentiators.
      • Technical Mastery: "AI-maxing" requires doubling down on mathematics and computer science to effectively prompt and direct AI models.
      • Distribution Mastery: "Looksmaxing/Vibe-maxing" focuses on building high-value human relationships and commanding public attention.
  • The Power Law of Creators: Top-tier creators like Jake Paul, Logan Paul, and MrBeast have achieved "escape velocity." In an era of infinite AI-generated noise, established, real-world human brands are highly durable and compound rapidly.
  • Charisma-Driven Distribution: Woo draws a contrast between MrBeast's analytical, format-driven content and Jake Paul’s narrative-driven content. While a format can be replicated, organic personal charisma is a highly defensible, low-cost customer acquisition tool.

Founder Psychology & "Pain Tolerance"

  • The VC Value of Resilience: Woo highlights that early startup success requires an immense tolerance for psychological pain. Jake Paul's career—enduring mainstream media backlash, public controversies, and physical boxing training—serves as a blueprint for founder resilience.
  • Calculated Risk-Taking: Success in highly competitive fields requires taking bold, public risks while avoiding "fatal" downside risks.
      • The Golden Rule: Push boundaries, but do not take risks that result in death, physical destruction, or incarceration.
  • Unpacking Trauma: Paul advocates for deep self-care and childhood trauma therapy to help leaders connect adult anxieties to childhood patterns, warning that therapy should be used for behavioral correction rather than falling into "victim loops."

Future Frontiers: Politics, Education Reform, and Sports

  • Political Ambitions: Over the next 5 to 10 years, Paul plans to lean heavily into American politics to combat what he views as dangerous socialist and communist cultural shifts. He expressed a long-term openness to running for elective office or actively backing reform-minded candidates.
  • Education System Overhaul: Paul identifies education as a key area of interest, criticizing the current US system for failing to update its curriculum since the 1930s.
      • Focus Areas: Shifting away from abstract concepts (e.g., the Pythagorean theorem) toward practical financial education, tax literacy, and backing innovative models like Alpha School.
  • Financial Literacy & Equity: Anti Fund supports the concept of "Trump accounts" (Invest America), which aim to grant young Americans equity stakes in private tech companies to teach compounding wealth early in life.
  • NCAA & NFL Aspirations: In a first-time public disclosure, Paul shared his goal of walking onto the Stanford University football team as a slot receiver post-boxing, with ultimate aspirations of entering the NFL draft.

Notable Quotes

"If you're literally fighting and punching people or getting punched in the face for a living, and then two, like the internet punching you... that's presidential, head-of-state level courage and resiliency." — Geoff Woo
"Everything you do has risk. Just don't take fatal risk. Like, just don't get yourself killed. Don't go to jail... as long as you take risks and calculated bets and keep compounding, you'll eventually end up somewhere good." — Jake Paul
"In a world where there's more and more capital, is attention more scarce? Can you control mindshare? You really can't name a better partner than Jake Paul in terms of wielding attention and driving cultural taste." — Geoff Woo
"I really like childhood trauma work that you can connect to why you behave certain ways as an adult. I think it's important to unpack those things and learn about it." — Jake Paul

Connecting that to why you behave certain ways as an adult is important. I think it's important to unpack those things and learn about them. Totally. And then you think about it at larger scales, right? How do you impact culture? How do you impact society? Yeah, I think a lot of it was—I mean, I think you just have to be entertaining. My brother and I were doing that, posting every single day, like 15-minute videos that took the whole day to film. So, just effort, first and foremost. There was entertainment in it, and then I think diversifying with characters. I basically created a daily reality show with all these people on it and all these crazy things happening. That was cutting-edge at the time. And then, just always being the visionary of what's working, what trends: "Okay, let's make a rap song," and then all of a sudden it's one of the generational anthems where younger kids know every single word of it. It's like, "Oh, music worked. Okay, let's make more songs, let's make a whole album, let's make a Christmas album." So, it was constantly just bits of iteration, spanning the full spectrum of entertainment. It was songs, comedy, and I would make scary videos to have the fear aspect of it. Then all of a sudden, I'm going into athleticism, which is boxing, and that whole side of entertainment as well. I think modern-day entertainers have to do it all. To your point, we went to MrBeast's place in North Carolina—or Jimmy's place, rather—and he gave us a tour and showed us how analytical the whole operation is. Was yours much more artistic, creative, or emergent, in terms of, "Oh, let's fuck around and find out"? How did you think about what to spend time on, in comparison to Jimmy's operation? Yeah, I think it's similar, but Jimmy is a numbers guy and is super analytical. Mine is definitely analytical, but not at the level that Jimmy is with how he does things. Mine is more creative and entertainment-focused, just kind of a feeling thing, like you're saying. But that's why, you know, Jimmy just maxed out the numbers on YouTube and did every little thing possible to hack the algorithm. It's interesting because there are a lot of people who will get big but not be super durable. You just mentioned you, your brother, and Jimmy as the ones that really stuck out, so I'm curious why some people get big for a period of time—I'll hear about them on TikTok or some other platform—but I don't hear about them years later. Yeah, I've seen the biggest stars in the space from day one just not stick around anymore. It's weird; it's really weird. There was a group called Magcon back in the day—they were like the Justin Biebers of the internet world, and actually, Shawn Mendes spun out of there. He was one who obviously made it really big. But it's just interesting. I think people maybe don't have that resilience. They get tired of working, making content, going to a new platform, or they lose motivation, whatever it might be. I think the people who are truly destined to do this, who love doing it and are talented at it, are the ones that last longer. And I think you almost have to diversify. We've gotten into politics, business, and growing brands, so you start to have this audience that follows you for multiple different things. Some people love my ranch YouTube videos and only know about that, but then I'm also on an a16z podcast where people in the tech world are hearing about what I'm doing, though they probably won't watch my TikToks. So I'm doing all these different things and growing audiences in all of these different worlds, and I think that's been the key. Then, I think also knowing how to monetize it and make money is a reason for us to continue to exist, because a lot of people in the space don't know how to actually pull cash out. They can have a lot of followers, but they don't have the cash, and cash is king. I think that's been another very important reason for the 13 or 14 years of being at the top of the influencer game. To me, Jake is a startup in himself, and in startup land, it's all power law. I think essentially, Jake has hit escape velocity. I think Jake gets even more powerful in this new era of AI-generated characters and infinite noise. Episode 55: Economic Myths with Jeff Yass - Co-Founder and Managing Director of Susquehanna

Generating Alpha Podcast(2.0K)·19:21·139 views·2026-06-22
Jeff YassCo-Founder and Managing Director, Susquehanna
TL;DRMeasuring the national debt against GDP misdiagnoses the US economy's health because it ignores America's massive $230 trillion in national wealth; the real threat to prosperity is not the debt itself, but policies that stifle the wealth-generating…
"golden goose" or...

TL;DR

Measuring the national debt against GDP misdiagnoses the US economy's health because it ignores America's massive $230 trillion in national wealth; the real threat to prosperity is not the debt itself, but policies that stifle the wealth-generating "golden goose" or reject market-driven voucher systems in education and welfare.

Redefining the US Debt Crisis: Debt-to-Equity vs. Debt-to-GDP

  • The GDP Misconception: Assessing US fiscal health purely by comparing the $31 trillion national debt to the $31 trillion GDP (a 100% ratio) is fundamentally flawed.
      • The Wealth Factor: Unlike other countries, US national wealth is roughly seven times its GDP, sitting at close to $230 trillion.
      • Stock Market Disparity: The US stock market is worth $80 trillion (more than 2.5 times GDP), whereas in Europe and China, the stock market-to-GDP ratio is closer to 1:1.
  • The Debt-to-Equity Reality: When measured against total national assets—the equivalent of a corporation's debt-to-equity ratio—US debt is only about 13% to 14% of its wealth, which is near a historic low.
      • The Earnings Analogy: Judging the US by GDP is like refusing to lend to someone who earns $100,000 but has $5 million in the bank. Wealth, not immediate earnings, determines solvency.
  • The Real Economic Threat: The danger is not the debt itself, but the potential destruction of the wealth-generating engine.
      • Government Waste: While the government wastes an estimated $2 trillion of its annual $7 trillion budget, the country will not go broke unless bad regulations and high taxes decimate the $230 trillion wealth base.

The Underappreciated Power of Consumer Surplus

  • The Extraction Myth: Critics often view wealth accumulation as a zero-sum game, operating on the assumption that billionaires actively extract wealth from the lower and middle classes.
  • The Concept of Consumer Surplus: In reality, the vast majority of value created by entrepreneurs is gifted back to the public in the form of time and money saved.
      • The Amazon Example: Jeff Bezos accumulated roughly $200 billion when Amazon was valued at $2 trillion. However, the consumer surplus Amazon generated—by saving consumers trillions of hours—is estimated to be worth at least $10 trillion. Bezos ultimately captured only about 2% of the total value he created.
      • The Tylenol Example: A consumer is willing to pay $100 to cure a severe headache but only pays $1 for Tylenol. The pharmaceutical company makes a tiny profit, while the consumer pockets $99 of "surplus" value.
      • The Uber Example: Uber provides immense societal utility and convenience, yet the company's actual profit is a tiny fraction of the overall value it has added to global transportation.

Busting the "Tax the Rich" Accounting Myths

  • Corporate vs. Personal Tax Liabilities: Political claims that ultra-wealthy founders pay "zero taxes" rely on deceptive accounting practices.
      • Indirect Contributions: While founders like Jeff Bezos may have low personal income tax liabilities because they hold unsold stock, they pay massive taxes through their corporations. If Amazon pays $30 billion in annual taxes, Bezos's personal share of that payment is effectively $6 billion.
  • The Life Cycle of Billionaire Wealth: Critics overlook the ultimate destination of billionaire capital.
      • The Buffett Example: Warren Buffett legally minimizes his tax burden to maximize the compounding of his capital, only to pledge his entire fortune to philanthropic causes (such as eradicing disease in Africa via the Gates Foundation). Attempting to confiscate this wealth beforehand simply diverts it to government entities looking to buy votes.

Solving Inefficiency with a Universal Voucher System

  • "Legalizing" the Education System: The most effective lever to relieve societal suffering and eliminate government waste in America is introducing free-market competition to schools.
      • The Cost Discrepancy: New York City spends approximately $43,000 per student annually, while top-tier private and Catholic schools achieve better results for less than half that cost.
      • The Voucher Solution: If parents were given a voucher to spend at any school of their choice, the remaining balance could be saved. By graduation, a child could have $300,000 banked for their future.
  • Why Government Inefficiency Persists: The government effectively hides the true costs of public systems. Because parents do not receive a direct invoice for $43,000 every school year, they remain unaware of the systemic waste.
  • A Voucher-Based Welfare State: While returning to the strict limitations of the 9th and 10th Amendments is politically unfeasible, the country can drastically reduce corruption and waste by transitioning all welfare programs to voucher systems.
      • The Universal Model: Just as the government uses food stamps (vouchers) instead of running grocery stores, it should issue vouchers for education and healthcare rather than operating the schools and hospitals directly.

Notable Quotes

"Our real wealth is so enormous that the debt really isn't a problem. It's roughly 13 or 14% of our wealth, which is, I believe, a historic low... What we do have to worry about is destroying the golden goose that creates the wealth." — Jeff Yass
"The typical number [of wealth captured by an entrepreneur] is somewhere around 10%. So most of the money, most of the wealth created is just given to the consumer because you bought something at a much lower price than you're willing to pay for." — Jeff Yass
"When [Elizabeth Warren] says [Bezos] pays zero taxes, if Amazon makes $100 billion a year and pays $30 billion in taxes, 20% of that or 6 billion a year is his... He's paying $6 billion a year through Amazon, not zero like Elizabeth Warren thinks." — Jeff Yass
"The government should not run the schools, they should just give out vouchers. The government should not run the medical system... they should just give everybody a voucher. Now that would be a welfare state, but at least it would be a vastly more efficient welfare state than the one that we have now." — Jeff Yass
Well, Jeff, I want to thank you for coming back on. You're my first repeat guest. So yeah, thanks for taking the time. Oh, it's my pleasure, Amir. Thrilled to be here. Well, like our last episode, I think this is going to be different from most episodes I've done. Instead of talking about your background, formative experiences, and stories, I think we'll talk about a few economic myths under two general umbrellas. One of those is budget deficits and what people consider the US debt crisis, and the other is consumer surplus and the US tax system. So, I want to kick it off. We talked a bit, and I know you have a nuanced way of thinking about what most people treat as the looming debt crisis in the US. National debt is now one-to-one with GDP, and I think a lot of people are a bit anxious and scared about where it goes from here, and if the US government might default. Why is measuring debt purely against GDP the wrong way to judge the US? Well, I think if we have a problem, you want to diagnose the problem correctly; otherwise, you obviously can't fix it. We have roughly $31 trillion in debt, and we have a GDP of roughly 31 trillion, which is where we get the 100% number from. But the United States, unlike other countries, has a wealth that is vastly superior to its GDP. Our wealth is probably seven times our GDP, close to $230 trillion. That's because our stock market is worth $80 trillion—or almost more than two and a half times our GDP—while other countries, particularly in Europe and China, have stock market values and GDPs that are roughly one-to-one. So, we're in a different situation than other countries. For a long time, comparing debt to GDP was a pretty good approximation to figure out if you had a debt problem. It no longer is, because our wealth is so enormous. If you were lending money to somebody and they said they made $100,000 a year and they wanted to borrow a million dollars, you'd be afraid to do it. But if you knew they had $5 million in the bank, you wouldn't be afraid to do it. You really wouldn't care what their earnings are, and the GDP is essentially your earnings. You'd only care what their real wealth is. So, our real wealth is so enormous that the debt really isn't a problem. It's roughly 13% or 14% of our wealth, which is, I believe, a historic low. Now, it's pretty much a historic high except for World War II if you measure it to GDP, but if you measure it to wealth, which is really the only thing that matters, it's at a very low number. The actual problem is that the wasteful spending we have is wasteful. We're spending $7 trillion when $5 trillion could have gotten the same job done. It's a shame to waste $2 trillion a year, but we're not going broke. The way we go broke is if that $230 trillion wealth gets annihilated and goes down to 100 trillion, which is about where it was 10 or 15 years ago. With bad policies, that can happen. But as long as we keep the growth engine going, and people are productive and making new businesses and creating wealth, we really don't have to worry. What we don't have to worry about is the debt. What we do have to worry about is destroying the golden goose that creates the wealth. Yeah. And I guess the point you're making is when you're analyzing a company—when someone wants to invest in a company—they don't just measure debt on the balance sheet against its current income. Income, in this sense, would be like GDP. Instead, you're going to measure debt against assets. And so the US's assets are far greater than its income. So, yeah, like you said, comparing it strictly to GDP is not a great way to make an assessment. That totally makes sense. Exactly. It's debt to equity, as you said. Yes. And I guess to your point about as long as the economy keeps growing and the stock market keeps growing, we're in a totally good place, if not a great place. Like you said, these numbers are some of the historical lows. I guess, what are these things that, if we limit them, can kind of kill the economy? What are these general policies or what have you that you think are somewhat limiting? Well, you can go back, and we might well see this in '29: regulation that makes it impossible for business to get started, or super high taxes that make it impossible for business to raise the capital to get started. Remember, compare us to Europe. We're roughly the same population as most of Europe, and we're 50% richer. So, it doesn't take a remarkable policy change to make us a lot, lot poorer. We could easily become Europe, and then we will be in trouble. And it seems like we were also talking about this, that a lot of people in the US—especially in this day and age where everyone has a social platform and can go and talk about these things—kind of have a misconception about this economic concept of consumer surplus. In the sense of, they think that people who make a lot of money are value-extractive from society, that they take money from people or whatever. What is the big myth around consumer surplus? What are most people who are vilifying billionaires not understanding about consumer surplus? Well, even Jeff Bezos, who's a bright fellow—I don't know if he misspoke or he didn't understand it—he would talk about how Amazon was worth $2 trillion and he only owned 10% of it. This was a while ago, so he had $200 billion and the other 1.8 trillion was made for investors. So he said, "I only took 10% of it, the other 1.8 trillion went to investors." And that's true, but the real value that Amazon added is what we call consumer surplus. It made life so much easier and saved us so much time that that number dwarfs the market capitalization of Amazon. I would say it's at least five times greater. That $10 trillion worth of wealth was created because of how many hours it saved us. If you give that a multiple of 20, it would come to something like $10 trillion. So, really, Bezos made $200 billion, and the world made over 10 trillion. He really only got 2% of it. And that's a more realistic number of what entrepreneurs do. I'd say the typical number is somewhere around 10%. So most of the money, most of the wealth created, is just given to the consumer because you bought something at a much lower price than you're willing to pay for. I think the easiest example is Tylenol. If you've ever had a headache and you had to pay Tylenol a dollar for two aspirin, I think you would have paid a lot more to get rid of that headache. Tylenol makes a little bit of money, not too much, and you don't have a headache. What you would pay that dollar versus having a headache—you might easily pay $100 not to get a headache. So the vast majority of the benefits go to the consumer, which leads you to policies that would say we want all these entrepreneurs creating these great things. We want more Amazons, we want more Tylenol, and to have a tax system and a culture that vilifies these people is absolutely upside down. And I think a lot of people also just go after especially people on Wall Street, in the sense of they don't see as much direct value add as businesses like Amazon and these kinds of things. But the truth is, with any kind of business, people wouldn't use it if it weren't beneficial. Even businesses like your own contribute to making markets more efficient and to this general greater good, or else they wouldn't be in business. The only reason these businesses still survive and thrive is because people actually use them because it's beneficial to them, which I think is a misconception a lot of people don't understand. And I guess as we're on this topic, do you think there are any other kind of misconceptions people have about the ultra-rich? I think a lot of people think they're out to get them, or out to manipulate and extract value from the middle and lower class. Are there any other misconceptions, or do you think this is the biggest one? I think it's sort of the biggest one. The big misconception is that, to many people, if Elon Musk has a trillion dollars, that means he took a trillion dollars from somebody else. It's like one big pizza pie, and this guy came in and took all the slices. That is really how many people view the world. So that's a misconception that's been going on since Karl Marx, and probably way before him. But I think the misconception—and people talk about that, you read editorials and stuff and they'll all make fun of the idea that it's a zero-sum game, so that's out there. It's out there intellectually, though not really out there politically. But intellectually, because it's difficult to measure, we really never talk about the consumer surplus, which is really most of the value added. Just think of Uber. They make some money, but they don't really make a lot. And just think how they changed life because you can do so many things now that Uber is so easily accessible. We just take it for granted. But guess what? It wasn't here 25 or 30 years ago. Someone had to invent it, and it just changed life. Just think how thankful you are that you have Uber all the time, and the company itself is profitable, but it's not that profitable. Its profitability is a fraction of the value that it added to the rest of the world. Yeah. And then, as you said, there is more value being created than destroyed, which is also why our economy is growing and has grown. We're in this very unique position, like you mentioned before, where the US stock market is somewhere around $80 trillion. In most other countries, you compare the stock market size and it's usually one-to-one with GDP or debt or whatever. But in the US, we've created so much consumer surplus and so much value that we're in this kind of position where our economy is so huge and thriving. And yeah, I think you also mentioned this before. We've talked a bit about how you think the current US tax system is broken in the sense of a lot of people go screaming, "Tax the rich, tax the rich!" What do you think is fundamentally broken about a progressive tax system when it comes to how we reward entrepreneurs for creating value, and for creating surplus? Well, besides just the philosophical problem, there's just a literal technical problem. Elizabeth Warren will say that Jeff Bezos pays no taxes because if he owns stock in Amazon and he doesn't sell it, he may have no personal tax liability for the year. He's building up a huge tax liability in the future as the stock goes up, but Amazon the corporation pays taxes. So Bezos doesn't write a check to the government saying, "Dear government, here's a check from Jeff Bezos." Amazon writes the check on his behalf. So when she says he pays zero taxes, if Amazon makes $100 billion a year and pays $30 billion in taxes, 20% of that, or 6 billion a year, is his. So he's paying $6 billion a year through Amazon, not zero like Elizabeth Warren thinks. Now, that's just a factual thing, not a philosophical thing. If Amazon was a private company, he would be paying $6 billion a year in taxes. He pays zero directly, but the company pays it for him. So what's the difference? That's the mega-myth. It's just irrefutable, and it's just nonsense to say that he doesn't pay taxes. We could at least disprove that without any philosophy; this is just accounting. And then the other myth is when you think of like Warren Buffett. He said, "I'm going to pay as little taxes as I possibly can." He did everything legal in the tax code to avoid it, and said, "At the end of my life, I'm going to donate it all to charity," which is more or less what he's done. He chose to give it to Bill Gates because Gates convinced him that if you want to eliminate human suffering, the best place to go is Africa and bring medicine, food, and vaccines to Africa. So that's what he did. For all this while we might be vilifying a billionaire, at the end, he threw it back into the ocean. He didn't do anything with it except try and help the poorest people live a happier, healthier life. And for that, he's vilified. They just forget to follow the whole stream of money. He didn't have a big blowout $100 billion party before he died; he gave it to Africa. Now, that makes me think he's a pretty good guy and a pretty valuable member of society. But to some, they want to confiscate that money, use it to buy votes for themselves, and they really don't give a shit about poor Africans. And especially in your case, I guess people know about the giving pledge and things like this, but there's also, from what I've read, you have some massive philanthropic endeavors. I guess, in your specific case, what do you care about? What do you give to? I know you care about school choice, but what are these things that you give to? Well, I find the biggest lever to relieve suffering in America is to legalize the education system. If there was competition in schools, kids would be so much happier, they'd be so much freer, they'd be so much better educated, and they'd do it at half the price. The example I often give is $43,000 per kid in New York City. The best Catholic schools cost less than half that. You can give a kid a voucher, he can go to the Catholic school, the charter school, or the private school, and you can put that saved money in a bank account. When he graduates, he'd have $300,000. That's how much waste and fraud there is in the education system. So you can eliminate this so-called affordability crisis in one fell swoop: just legalize education and take it away from this ridiculous government monopoly that just spends more and more and more and doesn't get any results. So I find that, in my philanthropy, it's about great ideas, but it's also about the idea times the probability that it's actually doable. And this, I think, is politically doable. I have other ideas that may be just as big, but they have zero chance of happening or very little chance, so I don't worry about them. This one, when you multiply the parlay—if school choice is proposed, then will it pass—yields the greatest benefit. For sure. And I guess, kind of to your points here, why aren't people doing this? What's the root cause of why the government, or why aren't people making these things happen? I think people have no idea what it costs. One of the things that government does very effectively is they don't send you a bill for school. When you go to seventh grade, you're not getting a bill of $43,000. It's all smushed in with other taxes, or other people pay it for you, and you don't know. It sneaks up on you. If they sent you a bill, you would go nuts, and people would change their behavior. But that's largely what keeps the fraud going. Yeah. There's a very good way of hiding it and spreading it out, and yes, this makes a lot of sense. That's why I want to connect the dots. It's like if you say to people, "We can find waste and fraud, and the deficit's going to go from 2 trillion to 1 trillion," people are like, "Well, good, good, I don't care. It doesn't affect my life." You have to give them that trillion dollars. And the idea we're trying to get through in school choice is that we're going to get rid of the waste and fraud, get you a better education, and give you the savings, so you're actually motivated to do it. Yeah. And I guess I want to kind of ask one final question to you. We've gone through some, we've kind of debunked some economic myths that a lot of the US believes in this day and age. And this is a question that you've actually recommended I ask my guests on the podcast, so I guess we'll start with this one. This will be the first. But all this being said, if you had to pick one—or your best idea—to make America freer or more prosperous as a country, what would it be? Well, the best idea, which has zero chance of happening—I'll go first with that—would be to listen to the 9th and 10th Amendments of the Constitution. They basically say if we didn't explicitly state that the government can do it, you can't do it. That's what built America. It kept the government small, and it kept us less corrupt than other governments. Now, that ain't happening. But I think we can convince people that the next time we're having a welfare program, just make it a voucher. There's no reason the government should run things. The government doesn't own the farms and the grocery stores; they give out vouchers for food stamps. The government should not run the schools; they should just give out vouchers. The government should not run the medical system or the healthcare system; they should just give everybody a voucher. Now, that would be a welfare state, but at least it would be a vastly more efficient welfare state than the one that we have now. And while it's far from a perfect solution, I think it's about the best that we can do. I would say voucher, voucher, voucher. I don't think anybody could be acting in good faith when they prefer a government-run system versus just a system where the government gives out a voucher. 100%. I couldn't agree more. Well, Jeff, it's been a pleasure doing this again with you. I'm super excited for people to hear and learn from this. And hopefully, at some point in the future, we do it again. Okay. Thanks, Amir. This was fun.

Anthropic’s Safety Superpower | Stratechery by Ben Thompson

Stratechery(15.5K)·17:04·1.4K views·2026-06-22
Ben ThompsonAuthor and Founder, Stratechery
TL;DRAnthropic’s recent clash with the U.S.
government over its "Fable" model highlights a deeper strategic shift where the company leverages "safety" to justify aggressive data harvesting, silent model degradation, and a play to control the user touchpoint at the expense of...

TL;DR

Anthropic’s recent clash with the U.S. government over its "Fable" model highlights a deeper strategic shift where the company leverages "safety" to justify aggressive data harvesting, silent model degradation, and a play to control the user touchpoint at the expense of traditional software competitors.

The Government Clash Over Fable and Mythos 5

  • The Export Control Directive: The U.S. government issued an abrupt export control directive restricting all foreign nationals (including Anthropic’s own foreign employees) from accessing Fable 5 and Mythos 5. This forced Anthropic to disable the models for all customers.
  • The Jailbreak Trigger:
      • The government's action was triggered by a "jailbreak" bypass method, reportedly flagged to the government by Amazon (a major Anthropic investor and inference provider).
      • Anthropic downplayed the vulnerability, claiming the jailbreak was narrow, non-universal, and only exposed simple, minor security flaws that other publicly available models could also identify.
  • Inevitable Friction: Whether or not Mythos 5 poses an immediate national security threat, conflict between frontier AI labs and the government is inevitable. Future models will possess increasingly dangerous capabilities, particularly as models are used to train their own successors.

The Economic Imperative: Replacing Software

  • The Value Chain Problem: Currently, the vast majority of AI financial value flows to hardware and compute providers (Nvidia, TSMC, SK Hynix). Frontier labs like Anthropic and OpenAI lose billions of dollars building leading-edge models that are quickly commoditized by open-source alternatives.
  • Owning the Touchpoint: To survive, frontier labs must move up the value chain to own the user touchpoint, positioning themselves to replace traditional software companies rather than just acting as back-end API infrastructure.
  • The "Token Capital" Counter-Strategy:
      • Microsoft CEO Satya Nadella has publicly warned against a future where a few central models "hollow out" entire industries.
      • Nadella advocates for enterprises to build a "learning loop" of "human capital" and sovereign "token capital," allowing companies to build agentic systems where they can easily swap out generalist underlying models without losing proprietary corporate memory.

The Data and Power Imperatives

  • Aggressive Data Harvesting:
      • To fuel critical reinforcement learning, Anthropic implemented a policy to retain all Fable usage data for 30 days, even for enterprise plans that previously guaranteed zero data retention.
      • While Anthropic claims they currently do not train on this data, the lack of third-party storage safeguards suggests they will eventually use it to train future models.
  • Silent Competitor Sabotage:
      • At launch, Anthropic quietly implemented safeguards that actively degraded Fable's performance if a user attempted to use it for frontier LLM development (such as pretraining pipelines, distributed training infrastructure, or ML accelerator design).
      • Although Anthropic walked back this silent degradation—opting instead to openly hand off these requests to Opus 4.8—the initial policy revealed a willingness to silently alter model behavior to achieve competitive and political goals.
  • Supply Chain Risk: This willingness to silently manipulate model outputs positions Anthropic as a potential supply chain risk for developers and governments alike.

The Safety Narrative as a Business Superpower

  • The Safety Justification: Anthropic frames its highly self-serving business maneuvers—restricting API access, implementing aggressive data retention, and degrading competitor capabilities—under the righteous banner of "safety."
  • The Apple Analogy: Just as Apple successfully frames monopolistic App Store policies as protective measures for user privacy, Anthropic leverages safety to consolidate its own power and control over the AI ecosystem.
  • Talent and Mission Alignment: Unlike OpenAI, which has suffered from internal ideological conflicts, Anthropic enjoys perfect alignment between its talent and its mission. They sell researchers on the noble task of building a "machine god" safely, making business-friendly policy decisions feel like moral necessities.

Notable Quotes

"Every company is going to have to build what I think of as human capital and token capital... A company should be able to switch out a “generalist” model without losing the “company veteran” expertise built into their learning system. This is the key “test” of your control and sovereignty in the era ahead." — Satya Nadella, CEO of Microsoft
"Anthropic, on the other hand, has perfect alignment between talent and mission and business. The company gets to sell to researchers the creation of a machine god, with the mantle of being the sort of person who cares about the dangers and is smart enough to navigate them on behalf of humanity. That every policy change that falls out of that happens to be great for business is the most beautiful coincidence in the world." — Ben Thompson
"What I fear, however, is that it is one thing to have people convinced they know best building a smartphone that I can take or leave; it’s considerably more concerning to have them building superintelligence that has the potential to rival or exceed the power of nation states, or merely massive corporations." — Ben Thompson
Anthropic’s Safety Superpower was published on Monday, June 15th, 2026. I’m sympathetic to the cynics who consistently characterize Anthropic’s public statements, particularly those surrounding their model releases, as scare-mongering for the sake of marketing. It was only two months ago that Anthropic announced Mythos Preview, a model that they said was too dangerous to make publicly available, thanks in particular to its advanced cybersecurity capabilities. Then, two months later, the company publicly released Fable, a version of Mythos with various safety guardrails. Fable is, in my limited experience, a very impressive model. It’s increasingly difficult to objectively evaluate models for anything other than coding performance, but there is subjective feel, and I found my interactions with Fable to be extremely impressive. It made other models, including GPT 5.5 and Opus 4.8, feel small and dumb. The two times I felt that way previously were with GPT-4 and Grok 4, both of which represented new generations in terms of base model size and complexity; my sense is that Fable is downstream of a new pre-train and the first of a new generation. To that end, I can certainly buy the case that Fable/Mythos is in fact more capable when it comes to identifying and exploiting security issues, and that Anthropic’s cautious roll-out was justified. The problem with publicly releasing models, however, is that guardrails can be jailbroken, and apparently that is exactly what happened shortly after the release. Anthropic vs. the U.S. Government, Again. What happened next is somewhat unclear. Anthropic wrote in a blog post: "The U.S. government, citing national security authorities, has issued an export control directive to suspend all access to Fable 5 and Mythos 5 by any foreign national, whether inside or outside the United States, including foreign national Anthropic employees. The net effect of this order is that we must abruptly disable Fable 5 and Mythos 5 for all our customers to ensure compliance. Access to all other Anthropic models will not be affected. We received the directive from the government today at pm Eastern. The letter did not provide specific details of its national security concern. Our understanding is that the government believes it has become aware of a method of bypassing, or “jailbreaking” Fable 5. We reviewed a demonstration of this specific technique being used to identify a small number of previously known, minor vulnerabilities. These vulnerabilities all appear relatively simple, and we have found that other publicly-available models are able to discover them as well without requiring a bypass." Anthropic went on to make the case that non-universal jailbreaks were inevitable and also narrow, and that there was no evidence of a universal jailbreak. The jailbreak that was found, meanwhile, appears to have been reported by Amazon, which is notable given Amazon is both an investor in Anthropic and a major provider of inference to the company. As I write this, Senior Anthropic staff are in Washington, D.C. seeking to resolve what they insist is a misunderstanding, and which White House officials are suggesting is insouciance by the company’s leadership to legitimate national security concerns. I don’t actually have much to add to the current conflict given how many facts are in dispute. What I am not surprised about is the fact that the conflict is happening. I already explained in Anthropic and Alignment why conflict between the U.S. government and Anthropic was inevitable. To that end, people who are arguing that Mythos isn’t powerful enough to warrant the government’s drastic action are missing the point: if it’s not powerful enough now, the next one will be, or the one after that, particularly now that models are increasingly useful in creating their successors. That, however, raises another question, one that seems to validate the cynics’ viewpoint: If Mythos is so dangerous, why even release Fable in the first place? And why fight with the government doing exactly what you claim to want? In fact, I think that Anthropic’s actions are quite understandable; what makes the company unique is how it justifies them, and it is those justifications that both give the cynics their fuel, and Anthropic its magic. The Economic Imperative. For the first few years of AI, the most economic value has flowed to compute. For obvious reasons: we don’t have enough supply to meet demand, which has meant skyrocketing prices. The biggest beneficiaries have been Nvidia, TSMC, and the memory makers (SK hynix, Samsung, and Micron). Anthropic and OpenAI, meanwhile, have collectively lost tens of billions of dollars building leading edge models that, once released, are distilled and commoditized by open source models, primarily from China. This represents the bear case for the labs: they never cover their costs because their differentiation is fleeting, while free alternatives become “good enough.” And I think it’s a legitimate one; a world where models are interchangeable is one where models are commodities, while most of the value flows elsewhere. Right now that's compute. But in the fullness of time, whenever we have enough compute, the most valuable place to be in the value chain will be the place that has always been the most valuable: owning the user touchpoint. To that end, it has long been clear to me that the frontier labs have the economic imperative to move closer to the user. If you own the user touchpoint, then you have meaningful lock-in. And the best way to own the user touchpoint is to be the canvas for everything they need to do. This, by extension, means that the frontier labs are on a collision course with software companies. It's software that owns the user touchpoint. And it’s in the frontier labs’ long-term interest to not simply be a commodity input into software, but to simply replace software outright. Software companies, meanwhile, are working to do the opposite. Satya Nadella laid out his vision for how companies should build on models in an essay on X: "Every company is going to have to build what I think of as human capital and token capital. Human capital comprises the knowledge, judgment, relationships, ingenuity, and pattern recognition of its people, while token capital is the firm’s AI capability it builds and owns. Importantly, human capital does not become less valuable as token capital grows; it only becomes more valuable." "I believe human agency will be the driver of token capital growth. Humans will set ambitious goals, connect dots across domains, build relationships, and recognize patterns that matter most. Without human direction, you have compute running in circles. This means the real opportunity is not in picking the best model, but instead in building a learning loop on top of models where human capital and token capital compound." "You can offload a task or even a job, but you can never offload your learning. The future of the firm is the ability to compound that learning across people and AI. This requires a new architectural approach where every business is able to build agentic systems that improve over time, while still retaining control over their IP. A company should be able to switch out a “generalist” model without losing the “company veteran” expertise built into their learning system. This is the key “test” of your control and sovereignty in the era ahead." Nadella set this vision off with a warning: "The last thing any of us want is a world where every company across every sector is ceding value to a few models that eat everything they see. If all the value is accrued by only a few models, the political economy will simply not tolerate it. There is no societal permission for an AI future that hollows out entire industries. Think about what happened in the first phase of globalization where entire industrial economies were hollowed out by outsourcing." "The GDP numbers looked fine on the surface, but the displacement was real and the consequences are still being felt. Let us not bring that dynamic into the AI era, with a small number of AI systems capturing all the economic returns, while entire industries find their knowledge commoditized right out from underneath them." Here’s the problem with that analogy: the globalization happened, and the industrial economies were hollowed out. There’s a possibility that this isn’t a warning but a prophecy; small wonder Nadella is raising the alarm given that Microsoft could be one of the casualties. And, by the same token, the economic imperative for the model makers is to accomplish exactly this. The Data Imperative. The models, not even Mythos, are yet at this point. What they need, beyond more compute, is more and better data. Model improvements increasingly come from reinforcement learning. Some of this can be generated synthetically, but the most powerful lever for a frontier lab is real world use. This, I think, is a major reason why both OpenAI and Anthropic offer their heavily subsidized subscription plans. Semianalysis recently estimated that a $200 plan gets you $8,000 worth of Claude tokens, and $14,000 worth of Codex tokens. Of course both are fighting for user and developer mindshare, but they are also fighting to have access to actual usage data to make their models better. Anthropic upped the ante in a major way with Fable, announcing that they would retain the data for all usage for 30 days, even for their enterprise plans that previously promised zero data retention. The company said they would not train on this data, but they didn’t put in any sort of safeguards to guarantee they wouldn’t do so in the future, like storing the data with a third party. If this policy change, whenever Fable is restored, doesn’t lead to a significant loss of customers, I suspect it’s only a matter of time until they start using the data. It’s simply too valuable to their end goals. Note also the virtuous cycle with moving up into user touchpoints: the more workflows that are done directly with Claude or Codex, the more data each company gets to feed back into their training, which makes their products that much more capable and useful, expanding the number of workflows they can serve, expanding their access to data. Nadella, in his essay, highlights the importance of this data, but naturally thinks it should be independent from the model: "Companies need to turn their workflows, domain knowledge, and accumulated judgment into AI systems that improve with each use. Private evals should capture whether a model is actually improving against outcomes that matter to the business, not just external benchmarks. Private reinforcement learning environments should let models grow stronger on real traces from inside the organization." "Its knowledge base makes institutional memory queryable and use of tokens more efficient. This loop becomes the new IP of the firm. I think of it as a hill climbing machine; and unlike most assets, it compounds. Every improved workflow generates better training signal, which accelerates the accumulation of tacit knowledge unique to the firm. The companies that build this early will have an advantage that is hard to replicate, regardless of any new individual model capability." What if, however, the companies that give in to Anthropic’s data policies get better results right now? Or what if existing companies resist, leaving the door open for new companies, or the model makers themselves, to outcompete them in the market? Anthropic is certainly putting the resolve Nadella is calling for to the test. The Power Imperative. The data retention policies around Fable/Mythos were, amazingly enough, not even the most controversial part of the launch. Rather, Anthropic said at launch that it would silently degrade Fable performance if it were used for LLM development. From the system card: "We have also added safeguards related to frontier LLM development. As discussed in Section 6.1 of our February 2026 Risk Report, we are concerned about the risks of accelerating the overall pace of AI development, though we remain uncertain about the severity of these risks. In particular, our concern is with, as we wrote then, “accelerating other AI developers in building powerful AI systems that pose similar risks to the ones ours pose, without necessarily having commensurate safeguards.”" "In light of the ability of recent models to accelerate their own development, we’ve implemented new interventions that limit Claude’s effectiveness for requests targeting frontier LLM development. For example, on building pretraining pipelines, distributed training infrastructure, or ML accelerator design. Using Claude to develop competing models already violates our Terms of Service, but enforcing this restriction through our safeguards avoids accelerating the actors most willing to violate these terms." "Unlike our interventions for cybersecurity, biology and chemistry, and distillation attempts, these safeguards will not be visible to the user. Fable 5 will not fall back to a different model. Instead, the safeguards will limit effectiveness through methods such as prompt modification, steering vectors, or parameter-efficient fine-tuning. These interventions will not affect the vast majority of coding work." "We estimate they will impact roughly 0.03% of traffic, concentrated in fewer than 0.1% of organizations. When these interventions are active, we expect them to have minimal behavioral impact on the model except to limit its effectiveness in developing frontier LLMs. Claude will still respond helpfully to user requests. We’ll continue to improve the precision of our detection methods following the launch of this model." Anthropic walked back this change: Fable will simply hand off LLM-related requests to Opus 4.8, and disclose this hand off to the user. But I think the initial policy was very illuminating. On one hand, I actually don’t begrudge Anthropic not wanting to help its competitors. On the other hand, what should be blisteringly clear is that Anthropic does not think that anyone else other than them should even be making frontier LLMs. What makes this policy all the more remarkable is the fact it was enacted only two months after Anthropic had that dispute with the Department of War. The latter wanted to use Claude for any legal use, while the former wanted more stringent controls around surveillance and autonomous weapons. What this degradation represented was both the capability and willingness of Anthropic to silently alter its models to achieve its policy preferences. In other words, Anthropic willfully validated some of its critics’ worst fears in terms of being a supply chain risk. The broader takeaway from that previous episode, however, is that Anthropic believes that they are the ones who should have final say over how Anthropic is used. Given that they think only they should be developing leading edge AI, they by extension think that only they should have final say over AI generally. When you further combine this realization with the company’s pronouncements about AI’s ability to conduct all economic activity, you realize that Anthropic’s leadership effectively wants to have power over everything and everyone. The Safety Story. Of course Anthropic would never put things so baldly. The story, rather, is safety. I expect Anthropic to increasingly expose their model’s capabilities to end users through endpoints, increasingly tailored to different workflows, even as they start to restrict the API. This replacement of software and restriction of access will be done in the name of safety, even as Anthropic fulfills their economic imperative of getting closer to end users. Anthropic’s explanation for their dramatic change in their data retention policy was safety. Specifically, the company claims that retaining all user data for 30 days is necessary to prevent the jailbreaks the U.S. government is worried about. I can certainly imagine a future where safety compels them to train on this data as well, to better protect against malicious usage. The entire Anthropic origin story is rooted in the founders’ belief that OpenAI wasn’t taking safety seriously enough. The company believes that only they can control AI, and that because they uniquely care about safety, they are justified in trying to control everyone else, up to and including the U.S. government. Here’s the thing about these safety justifications: I think they work because, to Anthropic, they aren’t justifications. The company really believes that they are the only ones who believe in super intelligence, and thus are the only ones who are sufficiently concerned about the dangers. That excuses decision after decision, policy after policy, and confrontation after confrontation that, to people on the outside, look like a bizarre combination of cynicism and naiveté. The contrast to OpenAI is massive. I think that one way to understand how and why OpenAI lost its lead is that, in the years following the release of ChatGPT, the company has been at war with itself internally as what used to be a research lab was suddenly seized with the burden of being the accidental consumer tech company. To the extent OpenAI solved that conflict, it was by bleeding huge amounts of talent to Anthropic in particular. Anthropic, on the other hand, has perfect alignment between talent and mission and business. The company gets to sell to researchers the creation of a machine god, with the mantle of being the sort of person who cares about the dangers and is smart enough to navigate them on behalf of humanity. That every policy change that falls out of that happens to be great for business is the most beautiful coincidence in the world. I respect this alignment, and I fear it. I respect it because it is so clearly effective. The closest analogy is probably Apple, which has always framed every self-serving action in the guise of doing right by users — and often they were. So it is with Anthropic. What I fear, however, is that it is one thing to have people convinced they know best building a smartphone that I can take or leave; it’s considerably more concerning to have them building superintelligence that has the potential to rival or exceed the power of nation states, or merely massive corporations. The history of brilliant people convinced they know what humanity needs is a sordid one, precisely because they have convinced themselves that their intentions are good, justifying actions that very much are not.

The Real State of the American Consumer w/ Three Evercore Analysts | The Real Eisman Playbook Ep 65

Steve Eisman(180.0K)·69:27·45.7K views·2026-06-22

TL;DR

The K-shaped recovery is evolving into a "limping" middle class, forcing a mass consumer migration away from high-priced fast food and legacy carb-heavy brands toward value-driven platforms like Walmart. Consequently, brands with execution power or luxury appeal (such as Walmart and Ralph Lauren) are thriving, while legacy food manufacturers, mismanaged retail giants like Nike, and the frozen housing market face severe structural headwinds.

The Cracking Middle Class & The Flight to Value

  • The Limping Middle Class: While high-income consumers (the top quintile driving nearly 40% of US retail spending) remain insulated by the wealth effect, the middle-income cohort has experienced growing financial stress over the past 12 months.
  • Fast Food Price Resistance: Lower-income consumers (earning under $50,000) are dropping out of the restaurant market. Fast food chains raised prices by roughly 40% post-COVID while wages rose only 24%, triggering severe consumer backlash and pushing average same-store sales growth to negative 1%.
      • Casual Dining Outperforms: Chains like Texas Roadhouse and Longhorn Steakhouse are winning share because they limited price hikes to roughly 20%, maintaining a stronger relative value proposition.
  • Walmart’s Trade-In Flywheel: Higher-income households (earning $150,000+) are actively trading down to Walmart, utilizing Walmart Plus for everyday essentials. Walmart, Costco, and Amazon now command a combined 24% of all US retail sales.
  • The Frozen Housing Market: Existing home sales are severely depressed, with roughly 30 million homes "locked up" due to sub-3.5% mortgage rates and stagnant capital gains tax thresholds. This housing freeze has caused a four-year decline in home improvement demand, dragging down the retail sector.

Brand Winners & Losers in Specialty Retail

  • Ralph Lauren's Premium Pivot: Ralph Lauren successfully engineered a brand turnaround by pulling back from promotional off-price channels (like TJ Maxx) and department store couponing, raising average unit prices by 60% since 2018. They have recaptured Gen Z and aspirational buyers by shifting marketing dollars to high-end lifestyle experiences, including the Polo Bar and Ralph’s Coffee.
  • Nike's Direct-to-Consumer Misstep: Nike over-pivoted to direct-to-consumer (D2C) e-commerce, damaging wholesale relationships and losing critical retail shelf space. This strategic vacuum allowed competitors like Hoka, On Running, Adidas, and New Balance (now an $8 billion brand) to rapidly capture market share. Turnaround efforts under returned veteran leadership are moving slowly.
  • Lululemon's Product Vacuum: Lululemon is struggling to sustain its premium market share after the departure of its lead designer. Rather than maintaining brand consistency, the company chased temporary product trends (e.g., belt bags), allowing newer competitors like Alo Yoga and Vuori to win over premium athletic wear consumers.
  • Department Store Stabilization: Macy's is successfully consolidating its retail footprint toward a highly profitable core of roughly 400 stores. Due to disciplined inventory management post-COVID and strong luxury performance from Bloomingdale's, mid-tier department stores are stabilizing and are no longer automatic short targets for investors.

Packaged Food Headwinds & Restaurant Turnarounds

  • A "Very Dark Time" for Legacy Food: Major legacy packaged food brands (such as Campbell Soup, Kraft Heinz, General Mills, and Conagra) are facing structural decline. Campbell Soup projects 2027 input cost inflation of up to 6%, which will be highly difficult to pass through to an inflation-weary consumer.
  • The Protein & Satiety Revolution: Consumers are rapidly shifting away from legacy carb-heavy products (e.g., sugary breakfast cereals) to protein-rich, satiety-focused foods.
      • The GLP-1 Effect: While GLP-1 weight-loss drugs act as a direct ~0.5% headwind to total food consumption, the broader cultural shift toward wellness, portion control, and protein is a massive threat to traditional food portfolios.
  • Restaurant Stock Outlooks:
      • Starbucks (Positive): Poised for recovery under new CEO Brian Niccol. Same-store sales grew 7% in North America last quarter, though the company must now translate these sales into meaningful earnings flow-through.
      • Chipotle (Neutral/Watch): Despite a 20% stock correction and executive turnover, Chipotle remains fundamentally strong. The deployment of automated kitchen throughput equipment in half of its stores by year-end is projected to add 2 to 4 percentage points to same-store sales.
      • Underperformers: Wendy's, Pizza Hut, and Olive Garden are heavily exposed to price-sensitive cohorts and face persistent traffic declines.

Big Box Operational Playbooks & Technology Integration

  • Home Depot vs. Lowe's: Home Depot is favored over Lowe's due to its early, aggressive investments in expanding its "Pro" (professional contractor) distribution network, effectively widening its addressable market. Lowe's is currently copying this playbook but carries significantly higher debt leverage (over 3x debt-to-EBITDA).
  • Auto Parts Normalization: Auto parts distributors (O'Reilly, AutoZone) remain highly resilient as the average age of the US vehicle fleet climbs to 13 years. However, their stocks are experiencing short-term corrections as same-store sales growth decelerates from peak pandemic levels back toward historic norms.
  • The Reality of Retail AI: While AI remains in its early stages, top-tier retailers are successfully deploying it in two primary areas:

1. Logistics Optimization: Using predictive modeling to streamline supply chains from Asia to US distribution centers.

2. Hyper-Personalized Marketing: Transforming online storefronts (such as Macy's) from static displays into personalized digital vending machines based on individual user data.

3. Agentic Shopping Resistance: Approximately half of consumers state they do not trust "agentic AI" to shop on their behalf—a skepticism that closely mirrors early-stage consumer resistance to mobile phone shopping twelve years ago.

***

Notable Quotes

"They stacked inflation people woke up to... They went 40% up on pricing. The average wage increase in that time was 24% or so. So they are paying the price for that today." — David Palmer, *Evercore ISI Food & Restaurants Analyst*
"What we've seen in the last couple years is I call it a limping E. The low end is under pressure, the high end is fine, especially with the wealth effect. And the middle is limping. That's where the pressure is being felt." — Greg Melich, *Evercore ISI Big Box Retail Analyst*
"Usually, I will tell you when a company tells you they're doing something that's not the core of the business, ignore it or run for your life... But Ralph Lauren turned just a little bit of a cool vibe like that [Polo Bar, Ralph's Coffee] into bringing in consumers in a three-dimensional way." — Michael Binetti, *Evercore ISI Department Stores & Specialty Retail Analyst*

Nikesh Arora on The Future of Token Costs | Memory Becoming the Moat & Why Enterprise AI Isn't Ready

20VC with Harry Stebbings(210.0K)·76:38·17.8K views·2026-06-22
Nikesh AroraChairman and CEO, Palo Alto Networks Harry StebbingsHost, 20VC
TL;DRPalo Alto Networks CEO Nikesh Arora predicts that token costs will plummet to one-tenth of current pricing within three to five years, driving a massive wave of G&A headcount reduction as opinionated AI applications replace traditional SaaS workflows.
To survive this...

TL;DR

Palo Alto Networks CEO Nikesh Arora predicts that token costs will plummet to one-tenth of current pricing within three to five years, driving a massive wave of G&A headcount reduction as opinionated AI applications replace traditional SaaS workflows. To survive this shift, enterprises must transition from superficial AI-washing to deep, context-aware agentic frameworks while aggressively securing new vectors like agent-to-agent communication.

The Frontier Model Divide: Breadth vs. Depth

  • The Consumer vs. Enterprise AI Split: Frontier model developers are chasing consumer market share where breadth is prioritized and the tolerance for false positives is high.
      • Consumers act as the "human-in-the-loop" to filter out hallucinations, allowing companies like OpenAI, Google, and Anthropic to prioritize fast user acquisition and post-training data generation over perfect accuracy.
  • The High Cost of Enterprise Autonomy: Enterprise applications have zero tolerance for false positives, particularly when deploying autonomous agents.
      • The Waymo Analogy: Waymo represents the premier agentic AI product today because it replaces a human driver. However, achieving this required tens of billions of dollars in edge-case training and highly proprietary data that cannot be scraped from the public internet.
      • A generic frontier model cannot simply be plugged into a vehicle or enterprise workflow to make high-stakes, autonomous decisions without deep, localized context.
  • Memory as the Ultimate Moat: Value in the AI stack is shifting toward localized memory and context. Frontier model companies are aggressively building memory architectures around user consumption.
      • Stating that orchestration layers are currently underfunded compared to models, Arora warns that enterprises risk becoming "model captive" if they rely on the model layer to store critical organizational memory and context.

Token Economics, Compute Scarcity, and Workforce Re-Architecture

  • The 10x Token Price Reduction: Arora predicts that token costs will fall to 1/10th of their current price over the next three to five years as compute efficiency increases and hardware optimizations mature.
  • Unprofitable Consumer Subsidies: Currently, over 50% of global compute is allocated to consumer AI queries (such as search and chat) that run at a loss. This scarcity-driven price pressure is passed directly to enterprises utilizing tokens for coding and specialized applications.
  • G&A Headcount Reduction: Within the next three years, corporate G&A headcounts (Finance, HR, and Marketing) are projected to decrease by up to 50%.
      • This shift will occur as static SaaS applications are replaced by opinionated AI applications that act as smart assistants, outputting recommendations and handling process management.
  • The Paradoxical Demand for Tech Talent: Despite overall corporate headcounts shrinking, organizations will require *more* high-end technical resources to build AI harnesses, manage proprietary data pipelines, and deploy frontier models.
  • Palo Alto’s Darwinian Talent Strategy: Palo Alto Networks has shifted its hiring strategy to recruit exclusively through hackathons.
      • By replacing their natural 2% monthly attrition with AI-savvy graduates, the company plans to organically transform 20% to 25% of its workforce into AI-fluent talent over the next 12 months.
      • Arora cautions against capping token budgets for top developers, noting that high-performing AI talent uses up to 20 times more tokens than average employees and will naturally migrate to organizations with the largest compute budgets.

Reimagining Cybersecurity in the Agentic Era

  • AI as a Dual-Use Cybersecurity Accelerant: While advanced AI enables offensive actors to scan and daisy-chain code vulnerabilities instantly, it also dramatically accelerates defensive remediation.
      • Palo Alto Networks used AI to discover code vulnerabilities in six weeks that would have historically taken five to six years of human labor.
  • The Human Guardrail in Patching: Automated AI defensive patching cannot yet run completely autonomously. Because defensive AI models also suffer from false positives, auto-deploying patches without human testing risks breaking critical enterprise infrastructure.
  • Securing the Agentic Perimeter: As enterprises deploy autonomous AI agents, securing agent-to-agent communication becomes the next major security frontier.
      • Palo Alto Networks recently acquired an "AI Gateway" startup to route, monitor, and govern agent traffic, allowing organizations to block rogue agent actions at a central firewall.
  • The "Tesla" vs. "Waymo" Product Strategy: When integrating AI, traditional enterprises should adopt a "Tesla approach" (gradually automating segments of the product line while keeping humans in the loop for edge cases) rather than a "Waymo approach" (waiting years to launch a fully autonomous, perfect product) or a legacy approach (superficial AI-washing).

Strategic Leadership and Corporate Governance

  • Sunk Cost Pitfalls in Mergers & Acquisitions: When evaluating acquisitions, leaders must separate the effort put into due diligence from the final decision.
      • Arora shares advice from a board member: "Go for a long walk and ignore all the effort you put in. If this walked in the door today with zero effort, and all you had to do was write the check, would you still take it?"
  • The Leverage of Being Willing to Walk Away: True negotiating leverage does not make a leader soft; it allows them to optimize outcomes by walking away from misaligned deals.
  • Top-Down AI Transformation via "AIAIO": Arora runs a bi-weekly convergent meeting called "AIAIO" (AI Input-Output) with his top 15 to 20 technical leaders to track token usage, evaluate agents, and foster Darwinian competition.
      • He emphasizes that corporate transformation must be driven from the top down, rejecting the trend of hiring academic "Chief AI Officers" who lack execution skills.

Notable Quotes

"In technology, you miss one trick, you can survive; you miss two tricks, you are partly impaled; you miss three tricks, you could be obsolete." — Nikesh Arora
"SaaS applications have no opinion; AI applications will have opinions. And that's a fundamental rethink we need from a workflow perspective." — Nikesh Arora
"Go for a long walk, ignore all the effort you put in... Because sometimes what happens is you confuse effort with wanting to get the outcome." — Nikesh Arora (quoting a board member)
"When you negotiate, if you are fully vested in the outcome, you fold... But if I say, 'it's going to be these terms or no terms; I'm going to walk away,' then it's a battle of wits. Being willing to walk away doesn't make you softer; it ensures you understand the pros and cons of what you're dealing with." — Nikesh Arora

I don't know, tens of billions of dollars. So, that's what it takes to take one use case and train the hell out of it. And that's the depth issue, right? Because you need the depth of the context, understanding, and intelligence in and around the model to make it useful for the truly agentic use case. Trump signs executive order investing in quantum computing

LiveNOW from FOX(5.6M)·37:33·3.0K views·2026-06-22
Donald TrumpPresident of the United States
TL;DRPresident Donald Trump signed two executive orders aimed at securing U.S.
leadership in quantum computing and transition federal networks to post-quantum cryptography by 2031, while also addressing critical national security, energy, and domestic policy updates. ...

TL;DR

President Donald Trump signed two executive orders aimed at securing U.S. leadership in quantum computing and transition federal networks to post-quantum cryptography by 2031, while also addressing critical national security, energy, and domestic policy updates.

Quantum Computing and Post-Quantum Security Orders

  • Five-Year Quantum Initiative: The first executive order launches a coordinated national effort to build a scientifically relevant, error-corrected quantum computer and develop quantum-enabled sensors and networks within the next five years.
      • Scientific and Defense Mandates: The Department of Energy (DOE) is tasked with leading the development of the computer, while the Department of Defense and DOE will collaborate on advanced quantum sensing and networking capabilities.
  • Transition to Quantum Cryptography: The second executive order directs federal agencies to transition their computer systems to quantum-resistant cryptography by 2031 to protect critical infrastructure and financial systems.
      • NIST and Commerce Leadership: The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) and the Department of Commerce will spearhead the rollout of these advanced security standards.
  • Private Sector Achievements & Commitments:
      • Google's Willow Chip: Alphabet's Ruth Porat highlighted Google's "Willow" chip, which can complete a computation in under five minutes that would take a classical supercomputer 10 septillion years (a 1 followed by 25 zeros).
      • IBM Infrastructure: IBM’s Arvind Krishna praised the Commerce Department's recent $2 billion quantum manufacturing and fab investments, committing to delivering operational systems during Trump's current term.

Geopolitics, Energy, and Military Actions

  • Hormuz Strait Energy Flows: Trump announced that oil and natural gas are flowing through the Strait of Hormuz at record pre-crisis levels, safeguarded by the U.S. military.
  • Iranian Economic Restraints: U.S. sanctions relief and unfrozen assets for Iran will be strictly tied to humanitarian purchases. Trump noted that Iran is required to purchase agricultural goods, including corn and soybeans, exclusively from U.S. farmers.
  • Operation Midnight Hammer: Reflecting on the one-year anniversary of the military operation, Defense Secretary Pete Hegseth and Trump highlighted the 37-hour bombing mission that severely degraded 87% of Iran's drone and missile manufacturing, alongside its air defenses, navy, and command structures.
  • Venezuelan Oil Imports: Trump reported a highly profitable relationship with Venezuela, stating the U.S. is importing significant quantities of oil to Houston and Louisiana, recouping past intervention costs multiple times over.

Regional Politics and NATO Burden-Sharing

  • NATO Financial & Military Critique: Trump criticized European allies—specifically Germany, Italy, and UK Prime Minister Keir Starmer—for failing to support the U.S. when requested, despite trillions of dollars in historical U.S. defense contributions to Europe.
  • Colombian Presidential Election: Trump congratulated Abelardo de la Espriella ("El Tigre") on winning the Colombian presidential election, attributing his surprise victory in part to Trump's public endorsement.

Domestic Infrastructure and Urban Crime

  • Reflecting Pool Vandalism: Trump addressed the damage to the Lincoln Memorial Reflecting Pool, confirming a 350-foot slit was sliced into the protective liner.
      • Law Enforcement Action: The National Park Service and local authorities have arrested five suspects, with five more currently under active investigation.
      • Cost Discrepancy: Trump defended the $10 million cost and two-month timeline of his restoration project, comparing it favorably to the Obama-Biden administration’s $147 million project, which remained closed.
  • Metropolitan Crime Reductions: Trump claimed federal interventions have resulted in a 78% drop in crime in Memphis and a 79% drop in New Orleans. He offered to enact similar federal measures in Chicago to eliminate its crime issues within three to four months if requested by Illinois Governor J.B. Pritzker.

Notable Quotes

"Quantum technologies represent the next generation of innovation across computing, sensing, and networking, with enormous significance for our country's economic growth, scientific research, and cybersecurity." — President Donald Trump
"What the Willow chip does is it enables us to do a computation in less than five minutes that previously, on the best supercomputer, would have taken 10 septillion years... It's about medical breakthroughs, nuclear fusion, material science." — Ruth Porat, President and Chief Investment Officer of Alphabet and Google
"The overwhelming military victory that has been had over Iran is what has set the conditions for them to be at the table with our negotiators, and we will ensure, whether it's through a deal or through the war department, that they never get a nuclear weapon." — Pete Hegseth, Secretary of Defense
